(1917)">Messines</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="Battle of Passchendaele">3rd Ypres (Passchendaele)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Malmaison" title="Battle of La Malmaison">La Malmaison</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cambrai_(1917)" title="Battle of Cambrai (1917)">1st Cambrai</a></li></ul>\n<p><b>1918</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Operation_Michael" title="Operation Michael"><i>Michael</i></a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Lys_(1918)" title="Battle of the Lys (1918)">Lys</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_the_Aisne" title="Third Battle of the Aisne">3rd Aisne</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="Second Battle of the Marne">2nd Marne</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days_Offensive" title="Hundred Days Offensive">Hundred Days</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Amiens_(1918)" title="Battle of Amiens (1918)">Amiens</a></li></ul></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n                <div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="Theatres_of_World_War_I" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:25.5em;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th scope="col" class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="background-color:#C3D6EF;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Campaignbox_World_War_I" title="Template:Campaignbox World War I"><abbr title="View this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_World_War_I" title="Template talk:Campaignbox World War I"><abbr title="Discuss this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_World_War_I&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template" style=";background-color:#C3D6EF;;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="Theatres_of_World_War_I" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><span style="line-height:1.6em">Theatres of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a></span></div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/European_theatre_of_World_War_I" title="European theatre of World War I">European</a></dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Western Front</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Eastern Front (World War I)">Eastern Front</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Italian_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Italian Front (World War I)">Italian Front</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Balkans_Campaign_(World_War_I)" title="Balkans Campaign (World War I)">Balkans</a></li></ul>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_theatre_of_World_War_I" title="Middle Eastern theatre of World War I">Middle Eastern</a></dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Campaign" title="Caucasus Campaign">Caucasus</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Persian_Campaign" title="Persian Campaign">Persia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_campaign" title="Mesopotamian campaign">Mesopotamia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sinai_and_Palestine_Campaign" title="Sinai and Palestine Campaign">Sinai and Palestine</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arab_Revolt" title="Arab Revolt">Arab Revolt</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/South_Arabia_during_World_War_I" title="South Arabia during World War I">South Arabia</a></li></ul>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/African_theatre_of_World_War_I" title="African theatre of World War I">African</a></dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/South-West_Africa_campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="South-West Africa campaign">South-West Africa</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Togoland_Campaign" title="Togoland Campaign">Togoland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kamerun_Campaign" title="Kamerun Campaign">Cameroon</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_I)" title="East African Campaign (World War I)">East Africa</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Military_operations_in_North_Africa_during_World_War_I" title="Military operations in North Africa during World War I">North Africa</a></li></ul>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Asian_and_Pacific_theatre_of_World_War_I" title="Asian and Pacific theatre of World War I">Asian and Pacific theatre</a></dt></dl>\n<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare_of_World_War_I" title="Naval warfare of World War I">Naval theaters</a></dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/U-boat_Campaign_(World_War_I)" title="U-boat Campaign (World War I)">U-boat</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_U-boat_campaign_of_World_War_I" title="Atlantic U-boat campaign of World War I">Atlantic Ocean</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare_in_the_Mediterranean_during_World_War_I" title="Naval warfare in the Mediterranean during World War I">Mediterranean</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Western Front</b> was the main <a href="/wiki/Theater_(warfare)" title="Theater (warfare)">theatre of war</a> during the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>. Following the outbreak of war in August 1914, the <a href="/wiki/German_Army_(German_Empire)" title="German Army (German Empire)">German Army</a> opened the Western Front by invading <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, then gaining military control of important industrial regions in <a href="/wiki/Third_Republic_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Republic of France">France</a>. The tide of the advance was dramatically turned with the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="First Battle of the Marne">Battle of the Marne</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Race_to_the_Sea" title="Race to the Sea">Race to the Sea</a>, both sides dug in along a meandering line of fortified <a href="/wiki/Trench_warfare" title="Trench warfare">trenches</a>, stretching from the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> frontier with France, which changed little except during early 1917 and in 1918.\n</p><p>Between 1915 and 1917 there were several offensives along this <a href="/wiki/Front_(military)" title="Front (military)">front</a>. The attacks employed massive <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> bombardments and massed <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a> advances. Entrenchments, machine gun emplacements, <a href="/wiki/Barbed_wire" title="Barbed wire">barbed wire</a> and artillery repeatedly inflicted severe casualties during attacks and counter-attacks and no significant advances were made. Among the most costly of these offensives were the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun" title="Battle of Verdun">Battle of Verdun</a>, in 1916, with a combined 700,000 casualties (estimated), the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a>, also in 1916, with more than a million casualties (estimated), and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="Battle of Passchendaele">Battle of Passchendaele</a> (Third Battle of Ypres), in 1917, with 487,000 casualties (estimated).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle199745\xe2\x80\x9358_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle199745\xe2\x80\x9358-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991361\xe2\x80\x93363_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds1991361\xe2\x80\x93363-12">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To break the deadlock of trench warfare on the Western Front, both sides tried <a href="/wiki/Technology_during_World_War_I" title="Technology during World War I">new military technology</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons_in_World_War_I" title="Chemical weapons in World War I">poison gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">tanks</a>. The adoption of better tactics and the cumulative weakening of the armies in the west led to the return of mobility in 1918. The German <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensive</a> of 1918 was made possible by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk_(Russia%E2%80%93Central_Powers)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Brest-Litovsk (Russia\xe2\x80\x93Central Powers)">Treaty of Brest-Litovsk</a> that ended the war of the Central Powers against Russia and Romania on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Eastern Front (World War I)">Eastern Front</a>. Using short, intense <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_bombardment" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurricane bombardment">"hurricane" bombardments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infiltration_tactics" title="Infiltration tactics">infiltration tactics</a>, the German armies moved nearly 100 kilometres (60 miles) to the west, the deepest advance by either side since 1914, but the result was indecisive.\n</p><p>The inexorable advance of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied</a> armies during the second half of 1918 caused a sudden collapse of the German armies and persuaded the German commanders that defeat was inevitable. The German government surrendered in the <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_11_November_1918" title="Armistice of 11 November 1918">Armistice of 11 November 1918</a>, and the terms of peace were settled by the  <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> in 1919.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1914">1914</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: 1914">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="War_plans_.E2.80.93_Battle_of_the_Frontiers"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_plans_\xe2\x80\x93_Battle_of_the_Frontiers">War plans \xe2\x80\x93 Battle of the Frontiers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: War plans \xe2\x80\x93 Battle of the Frontiers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Schlieffen_Plan" title="Schlieffen Plan">Schlieffen Plan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plan_XVII" title="Plan XVII">Plan XVII</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Frontiers" title="Battle of the Frontiers">Battle of the Frontiers</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG/220px-Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG/330px-Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG/440px-Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG 2x" data-file-width="891" data-file-height="689" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stabilization_of_Western_Front_WWI.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Western Front and the <a href="/wiki/Race_to_the_Sea" title="Race to the Sea">Race to the Sea</a>, 1914</div></div></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:309px;max-width:309px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:307px;max-width:307px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:French_bayonet_charge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/French_bayonet_charge.jpg/305px-French_bayonet_charge.jpg" width="305" height="180" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/French_bayonet_charge.jpg/458px-French_bayonet_charge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/French_bayonet_charge.jpg/610px-French_bayonet_charge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="506" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">French <a href="/wiki/Bayonet" title="Bayonet">bayonet</a> charge</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:307px;max-width:307px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:German_infantry_1914_HD-SN-99-02296.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/German_infantry_1914_HD-SN-99-02296.JPEG/305px-German_infantry_1914_HD-SN-99-02296.JPEG" width="305" height="111" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/German_infantry_1914_HD-SN-99-02296.JPEG/458px-German_infantry_1914_HD-SN-99-02296.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/German_infantry_1914_HD-SN-99-02296.JPEG/610px-German_infantry_1914_HD-SN-99-02296.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1089" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">German infantry on the battlefield, 7 August 1914</div></div></div></div>\n<p>At the outbreak of the First World War, the German Army, with seven field armies in the west and one in the east, executed a modified version of the <a href="/wiki/Schlieffen_Plan" title="Schlieffen Plan">Schlieffen Plan</a>, moving quickly through neutral Belgium to attack France, and then turning southwards to encircle the <a href="/wiki/French_Army" title="French Army">French Army</a> and trap it on the German border.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamiltonHerwig2003159_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamiltonHerwig2003159-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The Western Front was the place where the most powerful military forces in Europe, the German and French armies, met and where the war was decided.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStevenson200544\xe2\x80\x9345_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStevenson200544\xe2\x80\x9345-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Belgian neutrality had been guaranteed by Britain under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1839)" title="Treaty of London (1839)">Treaty of London, 1839</a>; this caused Britain to join the war at the expiration of its ultimatum at 11&#160;pm GMT on 4 August. Armies under German generals <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Kluck" title="Alexander von Kluck">Alexander von Kluck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_von_B%C3%BClow" title="Karl von B\xc3\xbclow">Karl von B\xc3\xbclow</a> attacked Belgium on 4 August 1914. <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a> had been occupied without opposition on 2 August. The first battle in Belgium was the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Battle of Li\xc3\xa8ge">Siege of Li\xc3\xa8ge</a>, which lasted from 5\xe2\x80\x9316 August. Li\xc3\xa8ge was well fortified and surprised the German Army under B\xc3\xbclow with its level of resistance. German heavy artillery was able to demolish the main forts within a few days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffith20049_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffith20049-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Following the fall of Li\xc3\xa8ge, most of the Belgian field army <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_(military)" title="Withdrawal (military)">retreated</a> to <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antwerp_(1914)" title="Siege of Antwerp (1914)">Antwerp</a>, leaving the garrison of <a href="/wiki/Namur_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Namur (city)">Namur</a> isolated, with the Belgian capital, <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, falling to the Germans on 20 August. Although the German army bypassed Antwerp, it remained a threat to their flank. Another <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Namur_(1914)" title="Siege of Namur (1914)">siege</a> followed at Namur, lasting from about 20\xe2\x80\x9323 August.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198622\xe2\x80\x9324,_25\xe2\x80\x9326_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198622\xe2\x80\x9324,_25\xe2\x80\x9326-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The French deployed five armies on the frontier. The French <a href="/wiki/Plan_XVII" title="Plan XVII">Plan XVII</a> was intended to bring about the capture of <a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamiltonHerwig2003254_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamiltonHerwig2003254-17">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> On 7 August, the VII Corps attacked Alsace to capture Mulhouse and Colmar. The main offensive was launched on 14 August with the First and Second Armies attacking toward Sarrebourg-Morhange in Lorraine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths200330_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths200330-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In keeping with the Schlieffen Plan, the Germans withdrew slowly while inflicting severe losses upon the French. The French Third and Fourth Armies advanced toward the <a href="/wiki/Saar_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Saar River">Saar River</a> and attempted to capture Saarburg, attacking Briey and Neufchateau but were repulsed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198629\xe2\x80\x9330_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198629\xe2\x80\x9330-19">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The French VII Corps captured Mulhouse after a brief engagement on 7 August but German reserve forces engaged them in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mulhouse" title="Battle of Mulhouse">Battle of Mulhouse</a> and forced a French retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmithAudoin-RouzeauBecker200333_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmithAudoin-RouzeauBecker200333-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The German Army swept through Belgium, executing civilians and razing villages. The application of "collective responsibility" against a civilian population further galvanised the allies. Newspapers condemned the German invasion, violence against civilians and destruction of property, which became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Rape_of_Belgium" title="Rape of Belgium">Rape of Belgium</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorneKramer20011\xe2\x80\x93608_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorneKramer20011\xe2\x80\x93608-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> After marching through Belgium, Luxembourg and the <a href="/wiki/Ardennes" title="Ardennes">Ardennes</a>, the Germans advanced into northern France in late August, where they met the French Army, under <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Joffre" title="Joseph Joffre">Joseph Joffre</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Division_(military)" title="Division (military)">divisions</a> of the British <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_I)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War I)">Expeditionary Force</a> under <a href="/wiki/Field_Marshal" class="mw-redirect" title="Field Marshal">Field Marshal</a> <a href="/wiki/John_French,_1st_Earl_of_Ypres" title="John French, 1st Earl of Ypres">Sir John French</a>. A series of engagements known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Frontiers" title="Battle of the Frontiers">Battle of the Frontiers</a> ensued, which included the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Charleroi" title="Battle of Charleroi">Battle of Charleroi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mons" title="Battle of Mons">Battle of Mons</a>. In the former battle the French Fifth Army was almost destroyed by the German 2nd and 3rd Armies and the latter delayed the German advance by a day. A general Allied retreat followed, resulting in more clashes at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Le_Cateau" title="Battle of Le Cateau">Battle of Le Cateau</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Maubeuge" title="Siege of Maubeuge">Siege of Maubeuge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_St._Quentin_(1914)" title="Battle of St. Quentin (1914)">Battle of St. Quentin</a> (also called the First Battle of Guise).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETerraine200278\xe2\x80\x93175_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETerraine200278\xe2\x80\x93175-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Battle_of_the_Marne">First Battle of the Marne</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First Battle of the Marne">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="First Battle of the Marne">First Battle of the Marne</a></div>\n<p>The German Army came within 70&#160;km (43&#160;mi) of Paris but at the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="First Battle of the Marne">First Battle of the Marne</a> (6\xe2\x80\x9312 September), French and British troops were able to force a German retreat by exploiting a gap which appeared between the 1st and 2nd Armies, ending the German advance into France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrachan2001242\xe2\x80\x93262_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrachan2001242\xe2\x80\x93262-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The German Army retreated north of the <a href="/wiki/Aisne_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Aisne River">Aisne River</a> and dug in there, establishing the beginnings of a static western front that was to last for the next three years. Following this German retirement, the opposing forces made reciprocal outflanking manoeuvres, known as the <a href="/wiki/Race_for_the_Sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Race for the Sea">Race for the Sea</a> and quickly extended their trench systems from the <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss frontier</a> to the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198631\xe2\x80\x9337_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198631\xe2\x80\x9337-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The territory occupied by Germany held 64 percent of French <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig-iron production</a>, 24 percent of its <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">steel manufacturing</a> and 40 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal industry</a> \xe2\x80\x93 dealing a serious blow to French industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989265\xe2\x80\x936_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989265\xe2\x80\x936-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Entente side</a> (those countries opposing the German alliance), the final lines were occupied with the armies of each nation defending a part of the front. From the coast in the north, the primary forces were from Belgium, the British Empire and then France. Following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Yser" title="Battle of the Yser">Battle of the Yser</a> in October, the Belgian army controlled a 35&#160;km (22&#160;mi) length of <a href="/wiki/West_Flanders" title="West Flanders">West Flanders</a> along the coast, known as the <a href="/wiki/Yser_Front" title="Yser Front">Yser Front</a>, along the <a href="/wiki/Yser" title="Yser">Yser</a> river and the Yperlee canal, from <a href="/wiki/Nieuwpoort,_Belgium" title="Nieuwpoort, Belgium">Nieuwpoort</a> to <a href="/wiki/Boezinge" title="Boezinge">Boesinghe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartonDoyleVandewalle200517_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartonDoyleVandewalle200517-28">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the British Expeditionary Force (BEF) occupied a position on the flank, having previous occupied a more central position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196227_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196227-29">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Battle_of_Ypres">First Battle of Ypres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First Battle of Ypres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Ypres" title="First Battle of Ypres">First Battle of Ypres</a></div>\n<p>From 19 October until 22 November, the German forces made their final breakthrough attempt of 1914 during the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Ypres" title="First Battle of Ypres">First Battle of Ypres</a>, which ended in  a mutually-costly stalemate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrachan2001273\xe2\x80\x93278_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrachan2001273\xe2\x80\x93278-30">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> After the battle, <a href="/wiki/Erich_von_Falkenhayn" title="Erich von Falkenhayn">Erich von Falkenhayn</a> judged that it was no longer possible for Germany to win the war by purely military means and on 18 November 1914 he called for a diplomatic solution. The Chancellor, <a href="/wiki/Theobald_von_Bethmann-Hollweg" title="Theobald von Bethmann-Hollweg">Theobald von Bethmann-Hollweg</a>, <i>Generalfeldmarschall</i> <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a>, commanding <i><a href="/wiki/Ober_Ost" title="Ober Ost">Ober Ost</a></i> (Eastern Front high command), and Hindenburg\'s deputy, <a href="/wiki/Erich_Ludendorff" title="Erich Ludendorff">Erich Ludendorff</a>, continued to believe that victory was achievable through decisive battles. During the Lodz offensive in Poland <span class="nowrap">(11\xe2\x80\x9325 November),</span> Falkenhayn hoped that the Russians would be made amenable to peace overtures. In his discussions with Bethmann-Hollweg, Falkenhayn viewed Germany and Russia as having no insoluble conflict and that the real enemies of Germany were France and Britain. A peace with only a few annexations of territory also seemed possible with France and that with Russia and France out of the war by negotiated settlements, Germany could concentrate on Britain and fight a long war with the resources of Europe at its disposal. Hindenburg and Ludendorff continued to believe that Russia could be defeated by a series of battles which cumulatively would have a decisive effect, after which Germany could finish off France and Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoley2005105\xe2\x80\x93110_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoley2005105\xe2\x80\x93110-31">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1915">1915</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1915">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_front_1915-16.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Western_front_1915-16.jpg/220px-Western_front_1915-16.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Western_front_1915-16.jpg/330px-Western_front_1915-16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Western_front_1915-16.jpg/440px-Western_front_1915-16.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="946" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_front_1915-16.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Western Front, 1915\xe2\x80\x9316</div></div></div>\n<p>Between the coast and the <a href="/wiki/Vosges_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Vosges Mountains">Vosges</a> was a westward bulge in the trench line, named the <a href="/wiki/Noyon" title="Noyon">Noyon</a> salient for the captured French town at the maximum point of advance near <a href="/wiki/Compi%C3%A8gne" title="Compi\xc3\xa8gne">Compi\xc3\xa8gne</a>. Joffre\'s plan for 1915 was to attack the <a href="/wiki/Salients,_re-entrants_and_pockets" class="mw-redirect" title="Salients, re-entrants and pockets">salient</a> on both flanks to cut it off.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuller1992165_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuller1992165-32">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Fourth Army had attacked in Champagne from 20 December 1914&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 17 March 1915 but the French were not able to attack in Artois at the same time. The <a href="/wiki/Tenth_Army_(France)" title="Tenth Army (France)">Tenth Army</a> formed the northern attack force and was to attack eastwards into the Douai plain across a 16-kilometre (9.9&#160;mi) front between Loos and Arras.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeiberg2008110_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeiberg2008110-33">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> On 10 March, as part of the larger offensive in the Artois region, the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> fought the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Neuve_Chapelle" title="Battle of Neuve Chapelle">Battle of Neuve Chapelle</a> to capture Aubers Ridge. The assault was made by four divisions along a 2&#160;mi (3.2&#160;km) front. Preceded by a surprise bombardment lasting only 35 minutes, the initial assault made rapid progress and the village was captured within four hours. The advance then slowed because of supply and communication difficulties. The Germans brought up <a href="/wiki/Military_reserves" class="mw-redirect" title="Military reserves">reserves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counter-attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter-attack">counter-attacked</a>, forestalling the attempt to capture the ridge. Since the British had used about one-third of their supply of <a href="/wiki/Shell_(projectile)" title="Shell (projectile)">artillery ammunition</a>, General Sir John French blamed the failure on the <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">shortage of ammunition</a>, despite the early success.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons2000112_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons2000112-34">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuller1992166\xe2\x80\x937_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuller1992166\xe2\x80\x937-35">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_warfare">Gas warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Gas warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons_in_World_War_I" title="Chemical weapons in World War I">Chemical weapons in World War I</a></div>\n<p>All sides had signed the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_of_1899_and_1907" title="Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907">Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907</a>, which prohibited the use of chemical weapons in warfare. In 1914, there had been small-scale attempts by both the French and Germans to use various <a href="/wiki/Tear_gas" title="Tear gas">tear gases</a>, which were not strictly prohibited by the early treaties but which were also ineffective.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter19947_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter19947-36">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The first use of more lethal <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical weapons">chemical weapons</a> was against the French near the Belgian town of <a href="/wiki/Ypres" title="Ypres">Ypres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoughty2005148\xe2\x80\x93151_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoughty2005148\xe2\x80\x93151-37">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the German plans to maintain the stalemate with the French and British, <a href="/wiki/Albrecht,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Albrecht, Duke of W\xc3\xbcrttemberg">Albrecht, Duke of W\xc3\xbcrttemberg</a>, commander of the <a href="/wiki/4th_Army_(German_Empire)" title="4th Army (German Empire)">4th Army</a> planned an offensive at Ypres, site of the First Battle of Ypres in November 1914. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Ypres" title="Second Battle of Ypres">Second Battle of Ypres</a>, April 1915, was intended to divert attention from offensives in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Eastern Front (World War I)">Eastern Front</a> and disrupt Franco-British planning. After a two-day bombardment, the Germans released <a href="/wiki/Poison_gas_in_World_War_I#1915:_Large-scale_use_and_lethal_gases" class="mw-redirect" title="Poison gas in World War I">a cloud</a> of 168 long tons (171&#160;t) of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine gas</a> onto the battlefield. Though primarily a powerful irritant, it can <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiate</a> in high concentrations or prolonged exposure. Being heavier than air, the gas crept across <a href="/wiki/No_man%27s_land#World_War_I" title="No man&#39;s land">no man\'s land</a> and drifted into the French trenches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuller1992172\xe2\x80\x933_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuller1992172\xe2\x80\x933-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The green-yellow cloud started killing some defenders and those in the rear fled in <a href="/wiki/Panic" title="Panic">panic</a>, creating an undefended 3.7-mile (6&#160;km) gap in the Allied line. The Germans were unprepared for the level of their success and lacked sufficient reserves to exploit the opening. Canadian troops on the right drew back their left flank and halted the German advance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon201281\xe2\x80\x9395_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon201281\xe2\x80\x9395-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The gas attack was repeated two days later and caused a 3.1&#160;mi (5&#160;km) withdrawal of the Franco-British line but the opportunity had been lost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon201295\xe2\x80\x93121_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon201295\xe2\x80\x93121-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The success of this attack would not be repeated, as the Allies countered by introducing gas masks and other <a href="/wiki/Countermeasure" title="Countermeasure">countermeasures</a>. An example of the success of these measures came a year later, on 27 April in the <a href="/wiki/Gas_attacks_at_Hulluch" title="Gas attacks at Hulluch">Gas attacks at Hulluch</a> 40&#160;km (25&#160;mi) to the south of Ypres, where the <a href="/wiki/16th_(Irish)_Division" title="16th (Irish) Division">16th (Irish) Division</a> withstood several German gas attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones200222\xe2\x80\x9323_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones200222\xe2\x80\x9323-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The British retaliated, developing their own chlorine gas and using it at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Loos" title="Battle of Loos">Battle of Loos</a> in September 1915. Fickle winds and inexperience led to more British casualties from the gas than German.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter199469\xe2\x80\x9373,_88_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter199469\xe2\x80\x9373,_88-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> French, British and German forces all escalated the use of gas attacks through the rest of the war, developing the more deadly <a href="/wiki/Phosgene" title="Phosgene">phosgene</a> gas in 1915, then the infamous <a href="/wiki/Mustard_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustard gas">mustard gas</a> in 1917, which could linger for days and could kill slowly and painfully. Countermeasures also improved and the stalemate continued.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter1994182\xe2\x80\x93183,_210\xe2\x80\x93211_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter1994182\xe2\x80\x93183,_210\xe2\x80\x93211-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_warfare">Air warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Air warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Specialised aeroplanes for aerial combat were introduced in 1915. Aircraft were already in use for scouting and on 1 April, the French pilot <a href="/wiki/Roland_Garros_(aviator)" title="Roland Garros (aviator)">Roland Garros</a> became the first to shoot down an enemy aircraft by using a machine-gun that shot forward through the propeller blades. This was achieved by crudely reinforcing the blades to deflect bullets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpick2002326\xe2\x80\x93327_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpick2002326\xe2\x80\x93327-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Several weeks later Garros force-landed behind German lines. His aeroplane was captured and sent to Dutch engineer <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Fokker" title="Anthony Fokker">Anthony Fokker</a>, who soon produced a significant improvement, the <a href="/wiki/Synchronization_gear" title="Synchronization gear">interrupter gear</a>, in which the machine gun is synchronised with the propeller so it fires in the intervals when the blades of the propeller are out of the line of fire. This advance was quickly ushered into service, in the <a href="/wiki/Fokker_E.I" title="Fokker E.I">Fokker E.I</a> (<i>Eindecker</i>, or monoplane, Mark 1), the first single seat <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter aircraft</a> to combine a reasonable maximum speed with an effective armament. <a href="/wiki/Max_Immelmann" title="Max Immelmann">Max Immelmann</a> scored the first confirmed kill in an <i>Eindecker</i> on 1 August.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWise1981349\xe2\x80\x93350_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWise1981349\xe2\x80\x93350-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Both sides developed improved weapons, engines, airframes and materials, until the end of the war. It also inaugurated the cult of the <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">ace</a>, the most famous being <a href="/wiki/Manfred_von_Richthofen" title="Manfred von Richthofen">Manfred von Richthofen</a> (the Red Baron). Contrary to the myth, <a href="/wiki/Antiaircraft_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiaircraft warfare">anti-aircraft fire</a> claimed more kills than fighters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranatsteinMorton200340_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranatsteinMorton200340-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spring_offensive">Spring offensive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Spring offensive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg/220px-Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg/330px-Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg/440px-Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="805" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Capture_of_Carency_aftermath_1915_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of <a href="/wiki/Carency" title="Carency">Carency</a> after it was recaptured by France</div></div></div>\n<p>The final Entente offensive of the spring was the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Artois" title="Second Battle of Artois">Second Battle of Artois</a>, an offensive to capture <a href="/wiki/Vimy_Ridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Vimy Ridge">Vimy Ridge</a> and advance into the Douai plain. The French Tenth Army attacked on 9 May after a six-day bombardment and advanced 5 kilometres (3&#160;mi) to capture Vimy Ridge. German reinforcements counter-attacked and pushed the French back towards their starting points because French reserves had been held back and the success of the attack had come as a surprise. By 15 May the advance had been stopped, although the fighting continued until 18 June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmithAudoin-RouzeauBecker200379\xe2\x80\x9380_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmithAudoin-RouzeauBecker200379\xe2\x80\x9380-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In May the German Army captured a French document at <a href="/wiki/La_Ville-aux-Bois" title="La Ville-aux-Bois">La Ville-aux-Bois</a> describing a new system of defence. Rather than relying on a heavily fortified front line, the defence was to be arranged in a series of echelons. The front line would be a thinly manned series of outposts, reinforced by a series of strongpoints and a sheltered reserve. If a slope was available, troops were deployed along the rear side for protection. The defence became fully integrated with command of artillery at the divisional level. Members of the German high command viewed this new scheme with some favour and it later became the basis of an elastic <a href="/wiki/Defence_in_depth" title="Defence in depth">defence in depth</a> doctrine against Entente attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997165_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997165-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELupfer19811\xe2\x80\x9336_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELupfer19811\xe2\x80\x9336-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the autumn of 1915, the "<a href="/wiki/Fokker_Scourge" title="Fokker Scourge">Fokker Scourge</a>" began to have an effect on the battlefront as Allied reconnaissance aircraft were nearly driven from the skies. These <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a> planes were used to direct gunnery and photograph enemy fortifications but now the Allies were nearly blinded by German fighters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198126\xe2\x80\x9327_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198126\xe2\x80\x9327-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  However, the impact of German air superiority was diminished by their primarily defensive doctrine in which they tended to remain over their own lines, rather than fighting over Allied held territory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffith1994155\xe2\x80\x93156_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffith1994155\xe2\x80\x93156-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autumn_offensive">Autumn offensive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Autumn offensive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In September 1915 the Entente allies launched another offensive, with the French <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Artois" title="Third Battle of Artois">Third Battle of Artois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Champagne" title="Second Battle of Champagne">Second Battle of Champagne</a> and the British at Loos. The French had spent the summer preparing for this action, with the British assuming control of more of the front to release French troops for the attack. The bombardment, which had been carefully targeted by means of <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">aerial photography</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey2004245_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey2004245-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> began on 22 September. The main French assault was launched on 25 September and, at first, made good progress in spite of surviving wire entanglements and machine gun posts. Rather than retreating, the Germans adopted a new <a href="/wiki/Defence-in-depth" class="mw-redirect" title="Defence-in-depth">defence-in-depth</a> scheme that consisted of a series of defensive zones and positions with a depth of up to 8.0&#160;km (5&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuels1995168\xe2\x80\x93171_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuels1995168\xe2\x80\x93171-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 25 September, the British began the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Loos" title="Battle of Loos">Battle of Loos</a>, part of the Third Battle of Artois, which was meant to supplement the larger Champagne attack. The attack was preceded by a four-day artillery bombardment of 250,000 shells and a release of 5,100 cylinders of chlorine gas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalazzo200066_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalazzo200066-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartesveldt200517_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartesveldt200517-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The attack involved two corps in the main assault and two corps performing diversionary attacks at Ypres. The British suffered heavy losses, especially due to machine gun fire during the attack and made only limited gains before they ran out of shells. A renewal of the attack on 13 October fared little better.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarner20004\xe2\x80\x9331_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarner20004\xe2\x80\x9331-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In December, French was replaced by General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Haig,_1st_Earl_Haig" title="Douglas Haig, 1st Earl Haig">Douglas Haig</a> as commander of the British forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiest2005xvii_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiest2005xvii-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1916">1916</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1916">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148,_Westfront,_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg" width="220" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="558" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R05148,_Westfront,_deutscher_Soldat_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German soldier on the Western Front in 1916</div></div></div>\n<p>Falkenhayn believed that a breakthrough might no longer be possible and instead focused on forcing a French defeat by inflicting massive casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons2000141_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons2000141-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> His new goal was to "bleed France white".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnox2007153_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnox2007153-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> As such, he adopted two new strategies. The first was the use of <a href="/wiki/Unrestricted_submarine_warfare" title="Unrestricted submarine warfare">unrestricted submarine warfare</a> to cut off Allied supplies arriving from overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHull2005295\xe2\x80\x93296_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHull2005295\xe2\x80\x93296-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The second would be attacks against the French army intended to inflict maximum casualties; Falkenhayn planned to attack a position from which the French could not retreat, for reasons of strategy and <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">national pride</a> and thus trap the French. The town of <a href="/wiki/Verdun" title="Verdun">Verdun</a> was chosen for this because it was an important stronghold, surrounded by a ring of forts, that lay near the German lines and because it guarded the direct route to Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoley2005207\xe2\x80\x93208_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoley2005207\xe2\x80\x93208-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Falkenhayn limited the size of the front to 5\xe2\x80\x936 kilometres (3\xe2\x80\x934&#160;mi) to concentrate artillery firepower and to prevent a breakthrough from a counter-offensive. He also kept tight control of the main reserve, feeding in just enough troops to keep the battle going.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964236\xe2\x80\x937_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964236\xe2\x80\x937-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In preparation for their attack, the Germans had amassed a concentration of aircraft near the fortress. In the opening phase, they swept the air space of French aircraft, which allowed German artillery-observation aircraft and bombers to operate without interference. In May, the French countered by deploying <i>escadrilles de chasse</i> with superior <a href="/wiki/Nieuport" title="Nieuport">Nieuport</a> fighters and the air over Verdun turned into a battlefield as both sides fought for <a href="/wiki/Air_superiority" class="mw-redirect" title="Air superiority">air superiority</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198140_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198140-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Verdun">Battle of Verdun</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Battle of Verdun">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun" title="Battle of Verdun">Battle of Verdun</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg/220px-Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg/330px-Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg/440px-Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4168" data-file-height="2960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soldats_Argonne_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French soldiers observing enemy movements</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun" title="Battle of Verdun">Battle of Verdun</a> began on 21 February 1916 after a nine-day delay due to snow and blizzards. After a massive eight-hour artillery bombardment, the Germans did not expect much resistance as they slowly advanced on Verdun and its forts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons2000143_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons2000143-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Sporadic French resistance was encountered. The Germans took <a href="/wiki/Fort_Douaumont" title="Fort Douaumont">Fort Douaumont</a> and then reinforcements halted the German advance by 28 February.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin200128\xe2\x80\x9383_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin200128\xe2\x80\x9383-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Germans turned their focus to <a href="/wiki/Le_Mort_Homme" title="Le Mort Homme">Le Mort Homme</a> on the west bank of the Meuse which blocked the route to French artillery emplacements, from which the French fired across the river. After some of the most intense fighting of the campaign, the hill was taken by the Germans in late May. After a change in French command at Verdun from the defensive-minded <a href="/wiki/Philippe_P%C3%A9tain" title="Philippe P\xc3\xa9tain">Philippe P\xc3\xa9tain</a> to the offensive-minded <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nivelle" title="Robert Nivelle">Robert Nivelle</a>, the French attempted to re-capture Fort Douaumont on 22 May but were easily repulsed. The Germans captured <a href="/wiki/Fort_Vaux" title="Fort Vaux">Fort Vaux</a> on 7 June and with the aid of <a href="/wiki/Diphosgene" title="Diphosgene">diphosgene</a> gas, came within 1 kilometre (1,100&#160;yd) of the last ridge before Verdun before being contained on 23 June.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonesHook200723\xe2\x80\x9324_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonesHook200723\xe2\x80\x9324-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over the summer, the French slowly advanced. With the development of the rolling <a href="/wiki/Barrage_(artillery)" title="Barrage (artillery)">barrage</a>, the French recaptured Fort Vaux in November and by December 1916 they had pushed the Germans back 2.1 kilometres (1.3&#160;mi) from Fort Douaumont, in the process rotating 42 divisions through the battle. The Battle of Verdun\xe2\x80\x94also known as the \'Mincing Machine of Verdun\' or \'Meuse Mill\'<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoley2005224_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoley2005224-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94became a symbol of French determination and self-sacrifice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson200128_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson200128-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_the_Somme">Battle of the Somme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Battle of the Somme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg/220px-British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg/330px-British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg/440px-British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5451" data-file-height="3834" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_infantry_Morval_25_September_1916.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British infantry advance near Ginchy. Photo by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Brooks_(photographer)" title="Ernest Brooks (photographer)">Ernest Brooks</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the spring, Allied commanders had been concerned about the ability of the French Army to withstand the enormous losses at Verdun. The original plans for an attack around the <a href="/wiki/River_Somme" class="mw-redirect" title="River Somme">River Somme</a> were modified to let the British make the main effort. This would serve to relieve pressure on the French, as well as the Russians who had also suffered great losses. On 1 July, after a week of heavy rain, British divisions in <a href="/wiki/Picardy" title="Picardy">Picardy</a> began the Battle of the Somme with the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Albert_(1916)" title="Battle of Albert (1916)">Battle of Albert</a>, supported by five French divisions on their right flank. The attack had been preceded by seven days of heavy artillery bombardment. The experienced French forces were successful in advancing but the British artillery cover had neither blasted away barbed wire, nor destroyed German trenches as effectively as was planned. They suffered the greatest number of casualties (killed, wounded and missing) in a single day in the history of the British Army, about 57,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198671\xe2\x80\x9372_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths198671\xe2\x80\x9372-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Verdun lesson learnt, the Allies\' tactical aim became the achievement of air superiority and until September, German aircraft were swept from the skies over the Somme. The success of the Allied air offensive caused a reorganisation of the German air arm and both sides began using large formations of aircraft rather than relying on individual combat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198142_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198142-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> After regrouping, the battle continued throughout July and August, with some success for the British despite the reinforcement of the German lines. By August, General Haig had concluded that a breakthrough was unlikely and instead, switched tactics to a series of <a href="/wiki/Military_unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Military unit">small unit</a> actions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196279_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196279-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The effect was to straighten out the front line, which was thought necessary in preparation for a massive artillery bombardment with a major push.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERawson2014140\xe2\x80\x93158_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERawson2014140\xe2\x80\x93158-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The final phase of the battle of the Somme saw the first use of the <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">tank</a> on the battlefield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1992245\xe2\x80\x93249_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1992245\xe2\x80\x93249-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The Allies prepared an attack that would involve 13 British and Imperial divisions and four French corps. The attack made early progress, advancing 3,200\xe2\x80\x934,100 metres (3,500\xe2\x80\x934,500&#160;yd) in places but the tanks had little effect due to their lack of numbers and mechanical unreliability.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson2005280\xe2\x80\x93281_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPriorWilson2005280\xe2\x80\x93281-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The final phase of the battle took place in October and early November, again producing limited gains with heavy loss of life. All told, the Somme battle had made penetrations of only 8 kilometres (5&#160;mi) and failed to reach the original objectives. The British had suffered about 420,000 casualties and the French around 200,000. It is estimated that the Germans lost 465,000, although this figure is controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatson200811_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatson200811-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Somme led directly to major new developments in infantry organisation and tactics; despite the terrible losses of 1 July, some divisions had managed to achieve their objectives with minimal casualties. In examining the reasons behind losses and achievements, once the British war economy produced sufficient equipment and weapons, the army made the platoon the basic tactical unit, similar to the French and German armies. At the time of the Somme, British senior commanders insisted that the company (120 men) was the smallest unit of manoeuvre; less than a year later, the section of ten men would be so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorkerry200188_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorkerry200188-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindenburg_line">Hindenburg line</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Hindenburg line">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_Line" title="Hindenburg Line">Hindenburg Line</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg/220px-Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg/330px-Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg/440px-Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1249" data-file-height="669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindenberg_line_bullecourt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hindenburg Line at <a href="/wiki/Bullecourt" title="Bullecourt">Bullecourt</a> seen from the air.</div></div></div>\n<p>In August 1916 the German leadership along the western front had changed as Falkenhayn resigned and was replaced by Hindenburg and Ludendorff. The new leaders soon recognised that the battles of Verdun and the Somme had depleted the offensive capabilities of the German Army. They decided that the German Army in the west would go over to the strategic defensive for most of 1917, while the Central powers would attack elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997246\xe2\x80\x93252_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997246\xe2\x80\x93252-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Somme battle and through the winter months, the Germans created a <a href="/wiki/Fortification" title="Fortification">fortification</a> behind the Noyon Salient that would be called the Hindenburg Line, using the defensive principles elaborated since the defensive battles of 1915, including the use of <a href="/wiki/Eingreif_division" title="Eingreif division">Eingreif divisions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynne1976290_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynne1976290-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> This was intended to shorten the German front, freeing 10 divisions for other duties. This line of fortifications ran from <a href="/wiki/Arras" title="Arras">Arras</a> south to <a href="/wiki/Saint-Quentin,_Aisne" title="Saint-Quentin, Aisne">St Quentin</a> and shortened the front by about 50 kilometres (30&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997246\xe2\x80\x93252_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997246\xe2\x80\x93252-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> British long-range reconnaissance aircraft first spotted the construction of the Hindenburg Line in November 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDockrillFrench199668_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDockrillFrench199668-79">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1917">1917</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 1917">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_Line" title="Hindenburg Line">Hindenburg Line</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tactical_development_on_the_Western_Front_in_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Tactical development on the Western Front in 1917">Tactical development on the Western Front in 1917</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Front_1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Western_Front_1917.jpg/220px-Western_Front_1917.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Western_Front_1917.jpg/330px-Western_Front_1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Western_Front_1917.jpg/440px-Western_Front_1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1242" data-file-height="961" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Front_1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Western Front, 1917</div></div></div>\n<p>The Hindenburg Line was built between 2<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964288\xe2\x80\x939_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964288\xe2\x80\x939-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and 50 kilometres (30&#160;mi) behind the German front line. On 25 February German forces began retreating to the line and the withdrawal was completed on 5 April, leaving behind a <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">devastated territory</a> to be occupied by the Allies. This withdrawal negated the French strategy of attacking both flanks of the Noyon salient, as it no longer existed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196299_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196299-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> However, offensive advances by the British continued as the <a href="/wiki/War_Office" title="War Office">High Command</a> claimed, with some justice, that this withdrawal resulted from the casualties the Germans received during the Battles of the Somme and Verdun, despite the Allies suffering greater losses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeiberg200446_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeiberg200446-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, on 6 April the United States declared war on Germany. In early 1915, following the sinking of the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania"><i>Lusitania</i></a>, Germany had stopped its unrestricted submarine warfare in the Atlantic because of concerns of drawing the United States into the conflict. With the growing discontent of the German public due to the food shortages, however, the government resumed unrestricted submarine warfare in February 1917. They had calculated that a successful submarine and warship siege of Britain would force that country out of the war within six months, while American forces would take a year to become a serious factor on the Western Front. The <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> and surface ships had a long period of success before Britain resorted to the convoy system, bringing a large reduction in shipping losses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986144\xe2\x80\x935_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986144\xe2\x80\x935-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front,_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front%2C_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg/220px-The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front%2C_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front%2C_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg/330px-The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front%2C_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front%2C_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg/440px-The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front%2C_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="635" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_on_the_Western_Front,_1914-1918_Q5925.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Army on the Western Front (August 1917)</div></div></div>\n<p>By 1917, the size of the British Army on the Western Front had grown to two-thirds the total numbers in the French forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989265\xe2\x80\x936_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989265\xe2\x80\x936-27">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In April 1917 the BEF began the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arras_(1917)" title="Battle of Arras (1917)">Battle of Arras</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196299\xe2\x80\x93100_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196299\xe2\x80\x93100-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Corps" title="Canadian Corps">Canadian Corps</a> and the <a href="/wiki/5th_Infantry_Division_(United_Kingdom)" title="5th Infantry Division (United Kingdom)">5th Division</a>, attacked German lines at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vimy_Ridge" title="Battle of Vimy Ridge">Vimy Ridge</a>, capturing the heights and the First Army to the south achieved the deepest advance since trench warfare began. Later attacks were confronted by German reinforcements defending the area using the lessons learned on the Somme in 1916. British attacks were contained and, according to Gary Sheffield, a greater rate of daily loss was inflicted on the British than in  "any other major battle".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield2014Chapter_7_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield2014Chapter_7-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the winter of 1916\xe2\x80\x931917, German air tactics had been improved, a fighter training school was opened at <a href="/wiki/Valenciennes" title="Valenciennes">Valenciennes</a> and better aircraft with twin guns were introduced. The result was near disastrous losses for Allied air power, particularly for the British, Portuguese, Belgians and Australians who were struggling with outmoded aircraft, poor training and weak tactics. As a result, the Allied air successes over the Somme would not be repeated and heavy losses were inflicted by the Germans. During their attack at Arras, the British lost 316 <a href="/wiki/Aircrew_member" class="mw-redirect" title="Aircrew member">air crews</a> and the Canadians lost 114 compared to 44 lost by the Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198171_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198171-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> This became known to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a> as <a href="/wiki/Bloody_April" title="Bloody April">Bloody April</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHart200511\xe2\x80\x9313_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHart200511\xe2\x80\x9313-87">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nivelle_Offensive">Nivelle Offensive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Nivelle Offensive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arras_(1917)" title="Battle of Arras (1917)">Battle of Arras (1917)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nivelle_Offensive" title="Nivelle Offensive">Nivelle Offensive</a></div>\n<p>The same month, the French <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">Commander-in-Chief</a>, General Robert Nivelle, ordered a new offensive against the German trenches, promising that it would end the war within 48 hours. The 16 April attack, dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Nivelle_Offensive" title="Nivelle Offensive">Nivelle Offensive</a> (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_the_Aisne" title="Second Battle of the Aisne">Second Battle of the Aisne</a>, after the area where the offensive took place), would be 1.2 million men strong, preceded by a week-long artillery bombardment and accompanied by tanks. The offensive proceeded poorly as the French troops, with the help of two <a href="/wiki/Russian_Expeditionary_Force_in_France" title="Russian Expeditionary Force in France">Russian brigades</a>, had to negotiate rough, upward-sloping terrain in extremely bad weather.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECockfield199991\xe2\x80\x93114_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECockfield199991\xe2\x80\x93114-88">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Planning had been dislocated by the voluntary German withdrawal to the Hindenburg Line. Secrecy had been compromised and German aircraft gained air superiority, making reconnaissance difficult and in places, the creeping barrage moved too fast for the French troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUffindell201526_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUffindell201526-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Within a week the French suffered 120,000 casualties. Despite the casualties and his promise to halt the offensive if it did not produce a breakthrough, Nivelle ordered the attack to continue into May.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196299\xe2\x80\x93100_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin196299\xe2\x80\x93100-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 3 May the weary French 2nd Colonial Division, veterans of the Battle of Verdun, refused orders, arriving drunk and without their weapons. Lacking the means to punish an entire division, its officers did not immediately implement harsh measures against the mutineers. <a href="/wiki/French_Army_Mutinies" class="mw-redirect" title="French Army Mutinies">Mutinies</a> occurred in 54 French divisions and 20,000 men deserted. Other Allied forces attacked but suffered massive casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyons2000243_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyons2000243-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Appeals to patriotism and duty followed, as did mass arrests and trials.  The French soldiers returned to defend their trenches but refused to participate in further offensive action.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964292_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964292-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> On 15 May Nivelle was removed from command, replaced by P\xc3\xa9tain who immediately stopped the offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeiberg200353_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeiberg200353-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The French would go on the defensive for the following months to avoid high casualties and to restore confidence in the French High Command, while the British assumed greater responsibility.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962101\xe2\x80\x93102_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962101\xe2\x80\x93102-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Expeditionary_Force">American Expeditionary Force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: American Expeditionary Force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 25 June the first US troops began to arrive in France, forming the <a href="/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Forces" title="American Expeditionary Forces">American Expeditionary Force</a>. However, the American units did not enter the trenches in <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="1st Infantry Division (United States)">divisional strength</a> until October. The incoming troops required training and equipment before they could join in the effort, and for several months American units were relegated to support efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986124_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986124-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In spite of this, however, their presence provided a much-needed boost to Allied morale, with the promise of further reinforcements that could tip the manpower balance towards the Allies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962144_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962144-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flanders_offensive">Flanders offensive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Flanders offensive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Messines_(1917)" title="Battle of Messines (1917)">Battle of Messines (1917)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Ypres" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Battle of Ypres">Third Battle of Ypres</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:At_close_grips2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/At_close_grips2.jpg/220px-At_close_grips2.jpg" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/At_close_grips2.jpg/330px-At_close_grips2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/At_close_grips2.jpg/440px-At_close_grips2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1834" data-file-height="1781" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:At_close_grips2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two British soldiers run toward a bunker past the bodies of two German
