soldiers</div></div></div>\n<p>In June, the British launched an offensive in Flanders, in part to take pressure off the French armies on the Aisne, after the French part of the Nivelle Offensive failed to achieve the strategic victory that had been planned and French troops <a href="/wiki/1917_French_Army_mutinies" title="1917 French Army mutinies">began to mutiny</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962101\xe2\x80\x93102_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962101\xe2\x80\x93102-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The offensive began on 7 June, with a British attack on <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Messines_(1917)" title="Battle of Messines (1917)">Messines Ridge</a>, south of Ypres, to retake the ground lost in the First and Second battles in 1914. Since 1915 specialist <a href="/wiki/Royal_Engineer_tunnelling_companies" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Engineer tunnelling companies">Royal Engineer tunnelling companies</a> had been digging tunnels under the ridge, and about 500&#160;t (490 long tons) of explosives had been planted in 21 mines under the German defences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBostyn2002227_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBostyn2002227-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Following several weeks of bombardment, the explosives in 19 of these mines were detonated, killing up to 7,000 German troops. The infantry advance that followed relied on three creeping barrages which the British infantry followed to capture the plateau and the east side of the ridge in one day. German counter-attacks were defeated and the southern flank of the Gheluvelt plateau was protected from German observation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199187_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds199187-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 11 July 1917, during <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hush" title="Operation Hush"><i>Unternehmen Strandfest</i></a> (Operation Beachparty) at Nieuport on the coast, the Germans introduced a new weapon into the war when they fired a powerful blistering agent <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_mustard" title="Sulfur mustard">Sulfur mustard</a> (Yellow Cross) gas. The artillery deployment allowed heavy concentrations of the gas to be used on selected targets. Mustard gas was persistent and could contaminate an area for days, denying it to the British, an additional demoralising factor. The Allies increased production of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons_in_World_War_I" title="Chemical weapons in World War I">gas for chemical warfare</a> but took until late 1918 to copy the Germans and begin using mustard gas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon200735\xe2\x80\x9336,_39_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon200735\xe2\x80\x9336,_39-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 31 July to 10 November the <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Ypres" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Battle of Ypres">Third Battle of Ypres</a> included the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="First Battle of Passchendaele">First Battle of Passchendaele</a> and culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Passchendaele" title="Second Battle of Passchendaele">Second Battle of Passchendaele</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddle2013112_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddle2013112-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The battle had the original aim of capturing the ridges east of Ypres then advancing to Roulers and Thourout to close the main rail line supplying the German garrisons on the Western front north of Ypres. If successful the northern armies were then to capture the German submarine bases on the Belgian coast. It was later restricted to advancing the British Army onto the ridges around Ypres, as the unusually wet weather slowed British progress. The Canadian Corps relieved the <a href="/wiki/II_ANZAC_Corps" title="II ANZAC Corps">II ANZAC Corps</a> and took the village of Passchendaele on 6 November,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962103_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962103-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> despite rain, mud and many casualties. The offensive was costly in manpower for both sides for relatively little gain of ground against determined German resistance but the ground captured was of great tactical importance. In the drier periods, the British advance was inexorable and during the unusually wet August and in the Autumn rains that began in early October, the Germans achieved only costly defensive successes, which led the German commanders in early October to begin preparations for a general retreat. Both sides lost a combined total of over a half million men during this offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield2002216_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield2002216-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The battle has become a byword among some British revisionist historians for bloody and futile slaughter, whilst the Germans called Passchendaele "the greatest martyrdom of the war".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheldon2007vi,_316_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheldon2007vi,_316-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Cambrai">Battle of Cambrai</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Battle of Cambrai">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cambrai_(1917)" title="Battle of Cambrai (1917)">Battle of Cambrai</a></div>\n<p>On 20 November the British launched the first massed tank attack and the first attack using predicted artillery-fire (aiming artillery without firing the guns to obtain target data) at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cambrai_(1917)" title="Battle of Cambrai (1917)">Battle of Cambrai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles199113\xe2\x80\x9315,_32\xe2\x80\x9335_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles199113\xe2\x80\x9315,_32\xe2\x80\x9335-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The Allies attacked with 324 tanks (with one-third held in reserve) and twelve divisions, advancing behind a hurricane bombardment, against two German divisions. The machines carried <a href="/wiki/Fascine" title="Fascine">fascines</a> on their fronts to bridge trenches and the 13-foot-wide (4&#160;m) German tank traps. Special "grapnel tanks" towed hooks to pull away the German barbed wire. The attack was a great success for the British, who penetrated further in six hours than at the Third Ypres in four months, at a cost of only 4,000 British casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles199188_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles199188-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The advance produced an awkward salient and a surprise German counter-offensive began on 30 November, which drove back the British in the south and failed in the north. Despite the reversal, the attack was seen as a success by the Allies, proving that tanks could overcome trench defences. The Germans realised that the use of tanks by the Allies posed a new threat to any defensive strategy they might mount. The battle had also seen the first mass use of German <i><a href="/wiki/Stosstruppen" class="mw-redirect" title="Stosstruppen">stosstruppen</a></i> on the Western front in the attack, who used infantry <a href="/wiki/Infiltration_tactics" title="Infiltration tactics">infiltration tactics</a> to penetrate British defences, bypassing resistance and quickly advancing into the British rear.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1991176\xe2\x80\x93248_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1991176\xe2\x80\x93248-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1918">1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:279px;max-width:279px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:277px;max-width:277px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_front_1918_german.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Western_front_1918_german.jpg/275px-Western_front_1918_german.jpg" width="275" height="213" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Western_front_1918_german.jpg/413px-Western_front_1918_german.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Western_front_1918_german.jpg/550px-Western_front_1918_german.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1242" data-file-height="961" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Map of the final German offensives, 1918</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:277px;max-width:277px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-P1013-316,_Westfront,_deutscher_Panzer_in_Roye.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-P1013-316%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Panzer_in_Roye.jpg/275px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-P1013-316%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Panzer_in_Roye.jpg" width="275" height="190" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-P1013-316%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Panzer_in_Roye.jpg/413px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-P1013-316%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Panzer_in_Roye.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-P1013-316%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Panzer_in_Roye.jpg/550px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-P1013-316%2C_Westfront%2C_deutscher_Panzer_in_Roye.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="552" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/A7V" title="A7V">German tank</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roye,_Somme" title="Roye, Somme">Roye</a>, 21 March 1918</div></div></div></div>\n<p>Following the successful Allied attack and penetration of the German defences at Cambrai, Ludendorff and Hindenburg determined that the only opportunity for German victory lay in a decisive attack along the Western front during the spring, before American manpower became overwhelming. On 3 March 1918, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk_(Russia%E2%80%93Central_Powers)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Brest-Litovsk (Russia\xe2\x80\x93Central Powers)">Treaty of Brest-Litovsk</a> was signed and Russia withdrew from the war. This would now have a dramatic effect on the conflict as 33 divisions were released from the Eastern Front for deployment to the west. The Germans occupied almost as much Russian territory under the provisions of the Treaty of Brest-Litovsk as they did in the Second World War but this considerably restricted their troop redeployment. The Germans achieved an advantage of 192 divisions in the west to the 178 Allied divisions, which allowed Germany to pull veteran units from the line and retrain them as <i><a href="/wiki/Stormtrooper" title="Stormtrooper">sturmtruppen</a></i> (40 infantry and 3 cavalry divisions were retained for German occupation duties in the east).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997393\xe2\x80\x93397,400\xe2\x80\x93401_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997393\xe2\x80\x93397,400\xe2\x80\x93401-106">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Allies lacked unity of command and suffered from morale and manpower problems, the British and French armies were severely depleted and not in a position to attack in the first half of the year, while the majority of the newly arrived American troops were still training, with just six complete divisions in the line.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962139\xe2\x80\x93140_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962139\xe2\x80\x93140-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Ludendorff decided on an offensive strategy beginning with a big attack against the British on the Somme, to separate them from the French and drive them back to the channel ports.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962140_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962140-108">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlyon2006543_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlyon2006543-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The attack would combine the new storm troop tactics with over 700 aircraft,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurphy200569_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurphy200569-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> tanks and a carefully planned artillery barrage that would include gas attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962140\xe2\x80\x93141_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962140\xe2\x80\x93141-111">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlyon2006544\xe2\x80\x93545_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlyon2006544\xe2\x80\x93545-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="German_spring_offensives">German spring offensives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: German spring offensives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensive</a></div>\n<p>Operation Michael, the first of the German <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensives</a>, very nearly succeeded in driving the Allied armies apart, advancing to within shelling distance of Paris for the first time since 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964353\xe2\x80\x93357_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964353\xe2\x80\x93357-113">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the battle, the Allies agreed on unity of command. General <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Foch" title="Ferdinand Foch">Ferdinand Foch</a> was appointed commander of all Allied forces in France. The unified Allies were better able to respond to each of the German drives and the offensive turned into a battle of attrition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962141\xe2\x80\x93143_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962141\xe2\x80\x93143-114">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In May, the American divisions also began to play an increasing role, winning their first victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cantigny" title="Battle of Cantigny">Battle of Cantigny</a>. By summer, between 250,000 and 300,000 American soldiers were arriving every month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962146_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962146-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> A total of 2.1 million American troops would be deployed on this front before the war came to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETuckerRoberts20051254_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETuckerRoberts20051254-116">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The rapidly increasing American presence served as a counter for the large numbers of redeployed German forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962146_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldwin1962146-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allied_counter-offensives">Allied counter-offensives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Allied counter-offensives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="Second Battle of the Marne">Second Battle of the Marne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days_Offensive" title="Hundred Days Offensive">Hundred Days Offensive</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_11_November_1918" title="Armistice of 11 November 1918">Armistice of 11 November 1918</a></div>\n<p>In July, Foch began the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_the_Marne" title="Second Battle of the Marne">Second Battle of the Marne</a>, a counter-offensive against the Marne salient which was eliminated by August. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Amiens_(1918)" title="Battle of Amiens (1918)">Battle of Amiens</a> began two days later, with Franco-British forces spearheaded by Australian and Canadian troops, along with 600 tanks and 800 aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEkins201024_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEkins201024-117">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Hindenburg named 8 August as the "Black Day of the German army".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986155\xe2\x80\x93156_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986155\xe2\x80\x93156-118">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Italian 2nd Corps, commanded by General <a href="/wiki/Alberico_Albricci" title="Alberico Albricci">Alberico Albricci</a>, also participated in the operations around Reims.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19942,_31,_242,_245\xe2\x80\x93246_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdmonds19942,_31,_242,_245\xe2\x80\x93246-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> German manpower had been severely depleted after four years of war and its economy and society were under great internal strain. The Allies fielded 216 divisions against 197 German divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989266\xe2\x80\x93302_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy1989266\xe2\x80\x93302-120">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days_Offensive" title="Hundred Days Offensive">Hundred Days Offensive</a> beginning in August proved the final straw and following this string of military defeats, German troops began to surrender in large numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997426\xe2\x80\x93428_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997426\xe2\x80\x93428-121">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> As the Allied forces advanced, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Maximilian_of_Baden" title="Prince Maximilian of Baden">Prince Maximilian of Baden</a> was appointed as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany_(German_Reich)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancellor of Germany (German Reich)">Chancellor of Germany</a> in October to negotiate an armistice. Ludendorff was forced out and fled to <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997426\xe2\x80\x93428_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997426\xe2\x80\x93428-121">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The German retreat continued and the <a href="/wiki/German_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="German Revolution">German Revolution</a> put a new government in power. The Armistice of Compi\xc3\xa8gne was quickly signed, stopping hostilities on the Western Front on 11 November 1918, later known as <a href="/wiki/Armistice_Day" title="Armistice Day">Armistice Day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986163_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffiths1986163-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The German <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilhelm II of Germany">Imperial Monarchy</a> collapsed when <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Groener" title="Wilhelm Groener">General Groener</a>, the successor to Ludendorff, backed the moderate Social Democratic Government under <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Ebert" title="Friedrich Ebert">Friedrich Ebert</a>, to forestall a revolution like those in Russia the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997446_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig1997446-123">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_I" title="Aftermath of World War I">Aftermath of World War I</a></div>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em;">\n<caption>Military align="right">774,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Austria-Hungary</td>\n<td align="right">3,200</td>\n<td align="right"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;10,000</span></td>\n<td align="right">4,100\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Central Powers</b></td>\n<td align="right"><b>1,496,200</b></td>\n<td align="right"><b>3,216,000</b></td>\n<td align="right"><b>778,100</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Grand Total</b></td>\n<td align="right"><b>3,258,610</b></td>\n<td align="right"><b>8,372,920</b></td>\n<td align="right">\xe2\x80\x94\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The war along the Western Front led the German government and its allies to sue for peace in spite of German success elsewhere. As a result, the terms of the peace were dictated by France, Britain and the United States, during the 1919 <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a>. The result was the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>, signed in June 1919 by a delegation of the new German government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlyon2006743_&amp;_760_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlyon2006743_&amp;_760-127">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The terms of the treaty constrained Germany as an economic and military power. The Versailles treaty returned the border provinces of Alsace-Lorraine to France, thus limiting the coal required by German industry. The <a href="/wiki/Saar_(League_of_Nations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Saar (League of Nations)">Saar</a>, which formed the west bank of the Rhine, would be demilitarised and controlled by Britain and France, while the <a href="/wiki/Kiel_Canal" title="Kiel Canal">Kiel Canal</a> opened to international traffic. The treaty also drastically reshaped Eastern Europe. It severely limited the German armed forces by restricting the size of the army to 100,000 and disallowing a navy or air force. The navy was sailed to <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a> under the terms of surrender but was later <a href="/wiki/Scuttling_of_the_German_fleet_in_Scapa_Flow" title="Scuttling of the German fleet in Scapa Flow">scuttled</a>, under the order of German admirals, as a reaction to the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMassie2004787_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMassie2004787-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casualties">Casualties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Casualties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Casualties_of_World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Casualties of World War I">Casualties of World War I</a></div>\n<p>The war in the trenches of the Western Front left tens of thousands of maimed soldiers and war widows. The unprecedented loss of life had a lasting effect on popular attitudes toward war, resulting later in an Allied reluctance to pursue an aggressive policy toward <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdamthwaite198925\xe2\x80\x9326_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdamthwaite198925\xe2\x80\x9326-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Belgium suffered 30,000 civilian dead and France 40,000 (including 3,000 merchant sailors).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrey1991292_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrey1991292-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The British lost <span class="nowrap">16,829</span> civilian dead, <span class="nowrap">1,260 civilians</span> were killed in air and naval attacks, <span class="nowrap">908 civilians</span> were killed at sea and there were <span class="nowrap">14,661 merchant</span> marine deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEASB1922100_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEASB1922100-131">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis1993269_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1993269-132">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Another 62,000 Belgian, 107,000 British and 300,000 French civilians died due to war-related causes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHersch192747\xe2\x80\x9361_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHersch192747\xe2\x80\x9361-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_costs">Economic costs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Economic costs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/French_war_planning_1920%E2%80%931940" title="French war planning 1920\xe2\x80\x931940">French war planning 1920\xe2\x80\x931940</a></div>\n<p>Germany in 1919 was bankrupt, the people living in a state of semi-starvation and having no commerce with the remainder of the world. The Allies occupied the Rhine cities of Cologne, Koblenz and Mainz, with restoration dependent on payment of reparations. In Germany a <a href="/wiki/Stab-in-the-back_myth" title="Stab-in-the-back myth">Stab-in-the-back myth</a> (<i>Dolchsto\xc3\x9flegende</i>) was propagated by Hindenburg, Ludendorff and other defeated generals, that the defeat was not the fault of the \'good core\' of the army but due to certain left-wing groups within Germany who signed a disastrous armistice; this would later be exploited by nationalists and the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazi</a> party propaganda to excuse the overthrow of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a> in 1930 and the imposition of the Nazi dictatorship after March 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerwig199687\xe2\x80\x93127_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerwig199687\xe2\x80\x93127-134">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>France lost more casualties relative to its population than any other great power and the industrial north-east of the country was devastated by the war. The provinces overrun by Germany had produced 40 percent of French coal and 58 percent of its steel output.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChickeringF\xc3\xb6rster2000297_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChickeringF\xc3\xb6rster2000297-135">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Once it was clear that Germany was going to be defeated, Ludendorff had ordered the destruction of the mines in France and Belgium.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964460_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1964460-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> His goal was to cripple the industries of Germany\'s main European rival. To prevent similar German attacks in the future, France later built a massive series of fortifications along the German border known as the <a href="/wiki/Maginot_Line" title="Maginot Line">Maginot Line</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander2003180_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander2003180-137">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Front_(World_War_I)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"General Brusilov, situated on the Austro-German border, of the General Staff,  still managed to make available 4 special brigades to France, or 745 officers and 43,547 foot soldiers."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaurel2001&amp;sect;1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaurel2001&amp;sect;1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The losses for the two Russian brigades totaled 70 officers and 4472 soldiers killed, wounded or missing in action."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaurel2001&amp;sect;1_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaurel2001&amp;sect;1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a
